###begin article-title 0
###xml 58 74 58 74 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Escherichia coli</italic>
###xml 58 74 <span type="species:ncbi:562">Escherichia coli</span>
Contributions of the individual hydrophobic clefts of the Escherichia coli beta sliding clamp to clamp loading, DNA replication and clamp recycling
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
###xml 0 16 0 16 <!--CREATIVE COMMONS-->
CREATIVE COMMONSThis is an Open Access article distributed under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution Non-Commercial License () which permits unrestricted non-commercial use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original work is properly cited.
###end p 1
###begin p 2
###xml 16 32 16 32 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Escherichia coli</italic>
###xml 225 226 216 217 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 668 669 656 657 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 684 685 669 670 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 699 700 681 682 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 705 706 684 685 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 717 718 693 694 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 723 724 696 697 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 848 849 818 819 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 865 866 832 833 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 871 872 835 836 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 964 965 925 926 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 980 981 938 939 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 986 987 941 942 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 16 32 <span type="species:ncbi:562">Escherichia coli</span>
The homodimeric Escherichia coli beta sliding clamp contains two hydrophobic clefts with which proteins involved in DNA replication, repair and damage tolerance interact. Deletion of the C-terminal five residues of beta (betaC) disrupted both clefts, severely impairing interactions of the clamp with the DnaX clamp loader, as well as the replicative DNA polymerase, Pol III. In order to determine whether both clefts were required for loading clamp onto DNA, stimulation of Pol III replication and removal of clamp from DNA after replication was complete, we developed a method for purification of heterodimeric clamp proteins comprised of one wild-type subunit (beta+), and one betaC subunit (beta+/betaC). The beta+/betaC heterodimer interacted normally with the DnaX clamp loader, and was loaded onto DNA slightly more efficiently than was beta+. Moreover, beta+/betaC interacted normally with Pol III, and stimulated replication to the same extent as did beta+. Finally, beta+/betaC was severely impaired for unloading from DNA using either DnaX or the delta subunit of DnaX. Taken together, these findings indicate that a single cleft in the beta clamp is sufficient for both loading and stimulation of Pol III replication, but both clefts are required for unloading clamp from DNA after replication is completed.
###end p 2
###begin title 3
INTRODUCTION
###end title 3
###begin p 4
###xml 4 20 4 20 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Escherichia coli</italic>
###xml 56 60 53 57 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">dnaN</italic>
###xml 67 68 64 65 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B1">1</xref>
###xml 151 152 145 146 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B2">2</xref>
###xml 325 326 316 317 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 689 690 680 681 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B2">2</xref>
###xml 4 20 <span type="species:ncbi:562">Escherichia coli</span>
The Escherichia coli beta sliding clamp, encoded by the dnaN gene (1), is the founding member of the ubiquitous beta sliding clamp family of proteins (2). The beta clamp was initially identified based on its ability to tether the replicative DNA polymerase (Pol) to the DNA template, effectively increasing its processivity (3). However, it is now clear that sliding clamp proteins interact with a variety of proteins in addition to their cognate replicative Pols that act on DNA, and also play important roles in managing the activities of these proteins to coordinate initiation of DNA replication with elongation, as well as DNA repair, DNA damage tolerance and cell-cycle progression (2). Although several models have been proposed to describe the role(s) by which sliding clamps coordinate these processes, the precise mechanism(s) are still largely unknown.
###end p 4
###begin p 5
###xml 173 174 170 171 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B2">2</xref>
###xml 317 320 314 317 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B4 B5 B6 B7 B8">4&#8211;8</xref>
###xml 493 494 486 487 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B5">5</xref>
###xml 495 496 488 489 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B9">9</xref>
###xml 529 530 519 520 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B5">5</xref>
###xml 787 788 774 775 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B9">9</xref>
###xml 899 901 886 888 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B10">10</xref>
###xml 962 970 949 957 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 972 973 959 960 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B9">9</xref>
###xml 1011 1018 998 1005 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 1020 1022 1007 1009 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B11">11</xref>
###xml 1011 1018 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
In general, bacterial clamps (beta clamps) exist as stable homodimers, whereas eukaryotic and archaeal clamps (proliferating cell nuclear antigen, or PCNA) are homotrimers (2). Despite a lack of sequence similarity, crystallographic studies have revealed that their three-dimensional structures are nearly identical (4-8). The head-to-tail arrangement of the protomers forms a ring with two distinct surfaces. This ring has a positively charged core of alpha-helices that associates with DNA (5,9) and is flanked by beta-sheets (5). The so-called N-side bears the N-terminus, while the C-side contains the C-terminus, which is part of a hydrophobic cleft that serves as a common docking site for most of its partner proteins. A structure for the beta clamp on DNA was recently reported (9). In the crystal, the clamp makes specific contacts with DNA, consistent with computational modeling studies (10). These contacts play poorly defined roles in clamp loading in vitro (9), and are essential for viability of E. coli (11).
###end p 5
###begin p 6
###xml 69 70 69 70 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 97 98 97 98 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A</underline>
###xml 105 106 105 106 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A</underline>
###xml 138 139 138 139 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A</underline>
###xml 183 185 183 185 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B12">12</xref>
###xml 213 220 213 220 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 384 385 360 361 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 428 429 386 387 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 536 537 470 471 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 563 564 481 482 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 615 616 515 516 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 636 637 524 525 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 679 680 559 560 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 681 683 561 563 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B12">12</xref>
###xml 739 743 613 617 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">dnaX</italic>
###xml 746 751 620 625 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B13 B14 B15">13&#8211;15</xref>
###xml 796 800 668 672 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">dnaX</italic>
###xml 936 937 802 803 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 1131 1133 977 979 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B16">16</xref>
###xml 1207 1208 1043 1044 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 1209 1211 1045 1047 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B17">17</xref>
###xml 1403 1404 1219 1220 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 1427 1428 1227 1228 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">1</sub>
###xml 1447 1448 1233 1234 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 1610 1611 1393 1394 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 213 220 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
Sliding clamps are loaded around DNA by a conserved multi-subunit AAA+ ATPase (expanded group of ATPases Associated with various cellular Activities) referred to as the clamp loader (12). The complete form of the E. coli clamp loader, DnaX complex (also called gamma complex), is comprised of two copies of the tau protein, and one copy each of gamma, delta, delta', chi, and psi (tau2gammadeltadelta'chipsi). In addition to tau2gammadeltadelta'chipsi, alternative forms of DnaX differing in the stoichiometry of tau and gamma (i.e. tau3deltadelta'chipsi or gamma3deltadelta'chipsi), and/or lacking chipsi (i.e. tau3deltadelta' or gamma3deltadelta') are active in clamp loading (3,12). Both tau and gamma are expressed from the same gene (dnaX) (13-15). tau represents the full-length product of dnaX, and is 643 amino acids (aa) in length, while gamma, which is 431-aa long, is the result of a programmed ribosomal frame shift. The tau2, gamma, delta and delta' subunits associate with each other forming a pentameric ring with a partial opening between delta and delta' through which DNA can pass during clamp loading/unloading (16). The psi subunit interacts with chi, and also interacts with tau/gamma (3,17). The DnaX complex associates with two Pol III core complexes, each of which consists of a single alpha catalytic (Pol IIIalpha), epsilon proofreading and theta subunit to form Pol III* ([tau2gammadeltadelta'chipsi]1[alphaepsilontheta]2). The complex consisting of Pol III* in association with two beta clamps is referred to as Pol III holoenzyme (Pol III HE) and acts to replicate the chromosome (3).
###end p 6
###begin p 7
###xml 208 210 205 207 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B12">12</xref>
###xml 211 213 208 210 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B18">18</xref>
###xml 406 408 403 405 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B12">12</xref>
###xml 409 411 406 408 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B16">16</xref>
###xml 502 504 490 492 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B12">12</xref>
###xml 641 643 629 631 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B19">19</xref>
###xml 850 852 829 831 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B12">12</xref>
###xml 853 855 832 834 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B19">19</xref>
Similar to clamp loaders from other organisms, DnaX adopts an arc-shaped structure that is proposed to interact with and stabilize an open form of the beta clamp without dissociating the clamp into monomers (12,18). This form of the clamp, in which a single interface is broken, adopts a lock washer-like structure. The structure of the clamp loader matches the contour of both the open clamp and the DNA (12,16). A stable DnaX-beta complex requires that the tau/gamma subunits of DnaX first bind ATP (12). This complex then associates with nicked or primed DNA, ratcheting itself over the 3'-end of the primer like a screw cap on a bottle (19). Once properly positioned at the 3'-end, the tau/gamma subunits hydrolyze bound ATP, triggering the immediate dissociation of DnaX from both the clamp and DNA, leaving beta behind to close over the dsDNA (12,19).
###end p 7
###begin p 8
###xml 4 11 4 11 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 213 214 210 211 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S</sup>
###xml 215 216 212 213 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">D</sub>
###xml 262 264 259 261 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>
###xml 375 377 369 371 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B21">21</xref>
###xml 613 615 607 609 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B22">22</xref>
###xml 678 680 668 670 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B23">23</xref>
###xml 694 701 684 691 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 766 767 753 754 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">K</italic>
###xml 767 768 754 755 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">m</sub>
###xml 780 782 767 769 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B24">24</xref>
###xml 4 11 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 571 576 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 694 701 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
The E. coli beta clamp contains a hydrophobic cleft composed in part of C-terminal residues. This cleft interacts with a fairly well-conserved eubacterial clamp-binding motif (CBM) that is often reported to be QL[S/D]LF, but can be considerably more degenerate (20). Many binding partners make important contacts with the beta clamp in addition to the CBM/cleft interaction (21). Another important paradigm that has become evident in recent years is that sliding clamps do not merely recruit partners to DNA, but can also stimulate their catalytic activity. For example, human PCNA enhances base excision repair (22). In addition, interaction of eukaryotic Pol kappa with PCNA (23), as well as E. coli Pol IV with beta clamp serves to markedly reduce their apparent Km for dNTPs (24). In light of these findings, sliding clamps should be viewed as dynamic coordinators of DNA replication, repair, and damage tolerance.
###end p 8
###begin p 9
###xml 361 363 361 363 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B25">25</xref>
###xml 515 520 515 520 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26 B27 B28">26&#8211;28</xref>
###xml 648 650 648 650 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B25">25</xref>
###xml 651 653 651 653 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B27">27</xref>
###xml 1017 1022 1014 1019 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B29 B30 B31">29&#8211;31</xref>
###xml 616 621 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
An important yet unanswered question pertains to how different partners exchange with each other, or switch, on a clamp encircling DNA. This is a particularly important issue in light of the fact that clamp proteins, and the processes that they manage, are so widely conserved throughout evolution. One well-conserved process, termed translesion DNA synthesis (25), involves specialized Pols capable of replicating over lesions in the DNA. Most Pols capable of catalyzing TLS display low fidelity on undamaged DNA (26-28). Mismanagement of TLS Pol function is widely believed to contribute to mutations that lead to human diseases such as cancers (25,27). One area that has received considerable attention in recent years pertains to Pol switching at a lesion in the DNA. One particularly popular yet untested model, referred to as the 'toolbelt model', postulates that two different Pols simultaneously bind to the same dimeric beta clamp, with each Pol contacting a separate hydrophobic cleft in the dimeric clamp (29-31). In this model, the clamp acts like a toggle switch to regulate access of the different Pols to the DNA template while coordinating high fidelity replication with potentially error-prone TLS.
###end p 9
###begin p 10
###xml 51 52 51 52 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B9">9</xref>
###xml 158 166 158 166 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 168 170 168 170 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B32">32</xref>
###xml 290 292 287 289 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B33">33</xref>
###xml 293 295 290 292 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B34">34</xref>
###xml 350 352 335 337 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B32">32</xref>
###xml 353 355 338 340 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B35">35</xref>
###xml 614 616 587 589 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B32">32</xref>
###xml 731 738 700 707 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 766 768 732 734 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B32">32</xref>
###xml 1297 1298 1254 1255 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 1492 1493 1446 1447 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1579 1580 1526 1527 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 1789 1790 1722 1723 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1795 1796 1725 1726 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 1824 1825 1751 1752 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1859 1860 1783 1784 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 1911 1912 1832 1833 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1917 1918 1835 1836 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 2021 2029 1931 1939 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 2049 2050 1956 1957 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 2055 2056 1959 1960 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 2153 2161 2057 2065 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
Despite its reported ability to interact with DNA (9), the clamp nonetheless slides along double strand (ds) DNA with a half-life on circular DNA of >100 min in vitro (32). Due to this remarkable stability, beta clamps accumulate on DNA, particularly lagging strand during DNA replication (33,34). As there are only approximately300-600 clamps/cell (32,35), and a new clamp is required for replication of each of the approximately4000 Okazaki fragments (assuming an average length of 1000 bp), it is postulated that the clamp must be actively unloaded (i.e. recycled) from DNA during replication/repair synthesis (32). The DnaX complex, as well as the delta subunit of DnaX, which is present in excess over the other DnaX subunits in vivo, can unload beta from DNA (32). Taken together, these findings highlight the need to carefully regulate both the loading and the unloading of clamps in order to ensure that partner proteins, such as TLS Pols, cannot gain access to the DNA unless their function is required. The goal of the work discussed in this report was determine whether a single cleft in the dimeric beta clamp was sufficient for clamp loading, Pol III* replication, and unloading clamp from DNA. To help answer these questions, we generated a mutant form of the beta clamp, called betaC, which lacks its C-terminal five aa residues. Using gel filtration chromatography, surface plasmon resonance (SPR) and isothermal titration calorimetry (ITC), we demonstrated that a single beta+ clamp binds two delta subunits of DnaX with negative cooperativity. In contrast, betaC was severely impaired for interaction with delta, DnaX, and, albeit to a lesser degree, Pol IIIalpha. Using a method that we developed for purification of heterodimeric beta clamp proteins, we purified a beta+/betaC clamp comprised of one beta+ protomer in complex with one betaC protomer. Biochemical characterization of the beta+/betaC heterodimer revealed that it bound a single delta, and interacted normally with DnaX and Pol IIIalpha in vitro. Moreover, the beta+/betaC heterodimer was proficient for loading onto DNA, as well as stimulation of Pol III* replication in vitro. In contrast, both clefts were required for efficient unloading of clamp from DNA by either DnaX or delta. Taken together, these findings provide insight into models for how the beta clamp may act to coordinate the actions of its different partners on DNA, as well as how clamp loading and unloading, or recycling, may contribute to this coordination.
###end p 10
###begin title 11
MATERIALS AND METHODS
###end title 11
###begin title 12
Proteins
###end title 12
###begin p 13
###xml 9 10 5 6 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3</sub>
###xml 138 140 122 124 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B21">21</xref>
###xml 141 143 125 127 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B36">36</xref>
###xml 191 193 167 169 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B21">21</xref>
###xml 241 243 217 219 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B37">37</xref>
###xml 325 327 301 303 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B21">21</xref>
###xml 461 462 434 435 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 609 613 582 586 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">dnaN</italic>
###xml 679 683 652 656 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">dnaN</italic>
###xml 714 720 683 689 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">TAATGA</underline>
###xml 738 742 707 711 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">dnaN</italic>
###xml 772 778 737 743 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">TCATTA</underline>
The gamma3deltadelta'chipsi form of the DnaX clamp loader complex was reconstituted from the individually purified subunits as described (21,36). The alpha subunit of Pol III (Pol IIIalpha) (21), and single-strand DNA-binding protein (SSB) (37) were each purified as described previously. Pol III* was purified as described (21). All protein concentrations were determined by densitometry of Coomassie-stained SDS-PAGE, and/or their extinction coefficient. betaC lacks its C-terminal five residues, and was cloned using the Quickchange kit (Stratagene) to replace the codons for residues M362 and P363 in the dnaN gene with stop codons (underlined) using primers (Sigma Genosys) dnaNDelta362-6Top (5'-GGCTTATGTTGTCTAATGAATGAGACTG-3') and dnaNDelta362-6Bottom (5'-CAGTCTCATTCATTAGACAACATAAGCC-3').
###end p 13
###begin p 14
###xml 22 23 19 20 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 32 33 26 27 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 62 64 56 58 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B31">31</xref>
###xml 94 95 85 86 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 125 126 106 107 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 145 146 126 127 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">6</sub>
###xml 167 168 145 146 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 177 178 152 153 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 204 207 176 179 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMK</sup>
###xml 227 230 192 195 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMK</sup>
###xml 326 330 288 292 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">dnaN</italic>
###xml 395 396 357 358 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">6</sub>
###xml 462 464 424 426 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B38">38</xref>
###xml 864 865 820 821 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">6</sub>
###xml 1036 1037 992 993 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sub>
###xml 1078 1079 1034 1035 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">4</sub>
Untagged forms of beta+ and betaC were purified as described (31) using plasmids pJRC210 (beta+) and pJRCbetaDelta362-6 (betaC). N-terminally his6-tagged forms of beta+ and betaC were expressed from pbetaHMK and pbetaDelta362-6HMK, respectively, while the N-terminally myc-tagged form of the beta clamp was expressed from pMYCdnaN using strain BLR(DE3) (Novagen). The clamps expressed with a his6-tag also bear an N-terminal cAMP-dependent protein kinase motif (38). Following their induced expression for 3 h at 37degreesC by addition of 0.5 mM IPTG, cell pellets were resuspended in 50 mM Tris-HCl (pH 7.5) with 10% sucrose and lyzed by French press. The soluble fraction was precipitated with sequential 30 and 70% ammonium sulfate cuts, and the 70% pellet was resuspended in Buffer A [20 mM Tris-HCl (pH 7.5), 0.5 mM EDTA, 0.1 mM DTT and 10% glycerol]. The his6-tagged clamps were purified by cobalt chelating chromatography using an AKTA FPLC equipped with a 5 ml HiTrap chelating HP column (GE Healthcare) charged with 100 mM CoCl2 and equilibrated in Buffer B [50 mM NaPO4 (pH 6.8), 0.5 M NaCl, 10% glycerol and 5 mM imidizole]. Bound protein was eluted using a 100-ml gradient (20 column volumes) of 5-250 mM imidizole in Buffer B. The myc-tagged beta protein was also purified using an AKTA FPLC (GE Healthcare). Buffer was exchanged using a 5-ml HiTrap desalting column equilibrated in Buffer A containing 50 mM NaCl according to manufacturer's recommendations. The protein was then loaded onto a MonoQ HR 16/10 column and eluted with a 50-500 mM NaCl gradient. It was then passed over a 5-ml HiTrap heparin column in Buffer A containing 50 mM NaCl to remove nuclease contaminants. Myc-tagged beta was not retained on this column.
###end p 14

The structure of the betaC mutant was compared to that of beta+ in solution using circular dichroism spectroscopy. The absorption spectra shown for each clamp protein (Figure 1B) were an average of four separate scans in the ultraviolet range (165-240 nm), and were obtained using a Jansco J-710 spectropolarimeter with a 1 cm cell length. ###xml 1880 3200 1768 3052 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F2" position="float"><label>Figure 2.</label><caption><p textid="20">Purification of heterodimeric &#946; clamp proteins. Although representative results for the &#946;<sup>+</sup>/&#946;<sup>+</sup> heterodimer are shown, identical results were observed during purification of the myc-&#946;<sup>+</sup>/his<sub>6</sub>-&#946;<sup>C</sup> heterodimer (&#946;<sup>+</sup>/&#946;<sup>C</sup>).</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="gkp128f2"/></fig>
Simultaneous expression of both myc- and his6-tagged forms of the beta clamp within the same bacterial strain was achieved using the pCOLADuet-1 vector (Stratagene). The gene encoding the N-terminally myc-tagged beta+ was subcloned from pMYCdnaN into multiple cloning site-1 (MCS-1), and that for either the N-terminally his6-tagged beta+ (pbetaHMK) or betaC (pbetaDelta362-6HMK) was subcloned into MCS-2, resulting in plasmids pSKSP100 (beta+/beta+) and pSKSP101 (beta+/betaC), respectively. The differentially tagged beta clamps were co-expressed in BLR(DE3) cells (Novagen) following induction by 0.5 mM IPTG, resulting in three different forms of the clamp: myc-tagged homodimer, his6-tagged homodimer and a myc- and his6-tagged heterodimer. Cell pellets were resuspended in Buffer B and lysed by French press. The three forms of the clamp were resolved from each other by cobalt chelating chromatography using a 5-ml HiTrap chelating HP column (GE Healthcare) charged with 100 mM CoCl2. Bound protein was eluted using a 100-ml gradient (20 column volumes) of 5-250 mM imidizole in Buffer B. As summarized in Figure 2A, three distinct peaks were observed: peak 1 contained myc-tagged homodimer, which eluted at approximately70 mM imidizole; peak 2 contained the myc-tagged and his6-tagged heterodimer, which eluted at approximately90 mM imidizole; and peak 3 contained the his6-tagged homodimer, which eluted at approximately120 mM imidizole. The myc-tagged beta+/his6-tagged beta+ (beta+/beta+) and myc-tagged beta+/his6-tagged betaC heterodimers (beta+/betaC) purified identically. For simplicity, we will refer to the wild-type clamp as beta+ in the text that follows, regardless of whether it is his6-tagged (betahis), myc-tagged (betamyc) or myc and his6 tagged (beta+/beta+). The specific form of the clamp used in each experiment is noted in the legend to each figure. Figure 2.Purification of heterodimeric beta clamp proteins. (A) FPLC chromatography trace of a representative HiTrap cobalt chelating HP column run for myc-beta+/his6-beta+. Respective positions of peaks 1-3, as well as the type of clamp present in that peak (homodimeric or heterodimeric) are indicated. Myc-tagged clamps are blue, and his6-tagged clamps are red. (B) Western blot analysis of peaks 1-3. Aliquots of each peak were separated by 12% SDS-PAGE, and probed with either anti-myc (top panel) or anti-Penta*His antibody (bottom panel). The load (load), cobalt column flow through (FT), and purified myc- (betamyc) and his6-tagged beta+ clamp proteins (betahis) were included as controls. Representative SPR traces in which the his6-tagged subunit was captured on the chip surface using anti-Penta*His antibody (anti-Penta*His on chip), and anti-myc antibody was injected over the chip surface (C), or the myc-tagged clamp subunit was captured on the chip surface using anti-myc antibody and anti-Penta*His was injected (anti-myc on chip) (D) are shown. SPR analysis was performed as described in 'Materials and Methods' section. Although representative results for the beta+/beta+ heterodimer are shown, identical results were observed during purification of the myc-beta+/his6-betaC heterodimer (beta+/betaC).
###end p 19

###begin title 21
Gel filtration chromatography
###end title 21
###begin p 22
###xml 84 85 81 82 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 94 95 88 89 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
Size exclusion chromatography was used to evaluate interactions of the untagged beta+ and betaC proteins (2.5 muM as dimer) with Pol IIIalpha (1.5 muM) or the delta subunit of DnaX (5 muM). Proteins were incubated for 30 min at room temperature in 100 mul of Binding Buffer [20 mM Tris-HCl (pH 7.5), 10% glycerol, 2 mM DTT, 0.1 mM EDTA and 100 mM NaCl]. The 100 mul binding reactions were then injected onto a Superose 12 10/30 column (GE Healthcare) equilibrated in the same buffer at a flow rate of 0.25 ml/min at 4degreesC. Aliquots (80 mul) of the indicated fractions (200 mul) were analyzed by 12% SDS-PAGE and Coomassie staining.
###end p 22
###begin title 23
SPR
###end title 23
###begin p 24
All SPR experiments were carried out at 25degreesC using a BIAcore X biosensor instrument (GE Healthcare) with HBS-EP [10 mM HEPES (pH 7.4), 150 mM NaCl, 3 mM EDTA and 0.005% v/v Surfactant P20] as running buffer. Approximately 5000 response units (RUs) of either BSA free anti-Penta*His Antibody (Qiagen), or 9E10 monoclonal anti-myc antibody (Covance) were covalently immobilized to either the CM5 or C1 sensor chip surface (GE Healthcare) by amine coupling in both flow cells according to the manufacturer's recommendations. The antibody surface in flow cell 1 was used to capture the appropriately tagged form of the beta clamp, while antibody in the flow cell 2 was left free to monitor nonspecific interactions for background subtraction. Immediately following capture of the clamp in flow cell 1, the indicated concentrations of the each beta binding partner were injected over the chip for 2 min at a flow rate of 30 mul/min. To limit effects resulting from dissociation of clamp dimers tethered to the chip, the antibody surface was regenerated by an injection of 10 mM glycine-HCl (pH 1.5) for 1 min at 50 mul/min. This removed all clamps and interacting proteins from the antibody surface following each interaction cycle, allowing for capture of a fresh aliquot of beta dimer for each interaction that was analyzed.
###end p 24

###xml 1356 3820 1329 3542 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="26">Interactions of the &#946; clamp with the &#948; subunit of DnaX. (<bold>A</bold>) Representative SPR results analyzing interaction of &#948; (4 &#956;M) with various &#946; clamp proteins (&#8764;100 RU). Clamps examined include the his<sub>6</sub>-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup> homodimer (&#946;<sup>+</sup>), the his<sub>6</sub>-tagged &#946;<sup>C</sup> homodimer (&#946;<sup>C</sup>), the his<sub>6</sub>-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup>/myc-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup> heterodimer (&#946;<sup>+</sup>/&#946;<sup>+</sup>) and the his<sub>6</sub>-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup>/myc-tagged &#946;<sup>C</sup> heterodimer (&#946;<sup>+</sup>/&#946;<sup>C</sup>). Theoretical <italic>R</italic><sub>max</sub> values for binding of a single &#948; subunit (&#948;1), or for two &#948; subunits (&#948;2) are indicated by dashed lines. (<bold>B</bold>) Titration of &#948; with his<sub>6</sub>-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup>. Increasing concentrations of purified &#948; (0.1, 1, 10, 25, 50, 100, 250, 500, 750 and 1000 nM) were injected over &#8764;100 RU of his<sub>6</sub>-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup> captured on the anti-Penta&#8226;His chip surface. (<bold>C</bold>) Comparison of sensorgrams from low (25 nM) and high (1 &#181;M) concentrations of &#948; injected over &#8764;100 RU of his<sub>6</sub>-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup> captured on the anti-Penta&#8226;His chip surface from panel (B) highlighting differences in association (<italic>k</italic><sub>a</sub>) and dissociation rates (<italic>k</italic><sub>d</sub>). The amplitudes (<italic>R</italic><sub>max</sub>) of these SPR traces were normalized to each other to facilitate a direct comparison of the association and dissociation phases of the curves. (<bold>D</bold>) Titration of &#948; with &#946;<sup>+</sup>/&#946;<sup>C</sup>. Increasing concentrations of purified &#948; (10, 25, 50, 100 and 500 nM) were injected over &#8764;100 RU of &#946;<sup>+</sup>/&#946;<sup>C</sup> captured on the anti-Penta&#8226;His chip surface. (<bold>E</bold>) The double-injection experiment was performed as outlined in the cartoon (bottom). &#8764;100 RU of his<sub>6</sub>-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup> was captured on the anti-Penta&#8226;His chip surface (injection 1). Saturating levels (10 &#181;M) of &#948; were injected (injection 2), forming the &#946;&#8211;&#948;<sub>2</sub> complex (<italic>R</italic><sub>max</sub> approaching 100 RU). Flow was switched to buffer, allowing for the more loosely bound &#948; (&#948;2) to dissociate, resulting in a &#946;&#8211;&#948;<sub>1</sub> complex. Increasing concentrations of &#948; (0, 1, 10, 25, 50, 100, 250, 500, 750 and 1000 nM) were then injected over the &#946;&#8211;&#948;<sub>1</sub> complex (injection 3) to determine the binding affinity for the second &#948; (&#948;2). (<bold>F</bold>) Summary table of kinetic constants for various clamp&#8211;&#948; interactions derived from SPR results summarized in (A&#8211;E). Results shown for &#946;<sup>+</sup>&#8211;&#948; were determined using the his<sub>6</sub>-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup> homodimer, and were indistinguishable from those observed with &#946;<sup>+</sup>/&#946;<sup>+</sup>. An interaction of &#946;<sup>C</sup> with &#948; was not detected (nd).</p>

In control experiments, the relative retention of beta on the antibody chip was used to determine the stability of heterodimeric clamps as a function of time. Initially, >95% of each heterodimeric clamp contained both the myc- and his6-tags, and were readily captured on the chip surface using either the anti-myc or anti-Penta*His antibody. Since clamp dimers dissociate into monomers and reassociate into a mixture of heterodimer and homodimers with time, we utilized an SPR assay to measure the level of each clamp captured by the tag-specific antibody over time. Results of these experiments indicated that the heterodimers were stable in the high salt buffer (Buffer B) in which they were purified for >180 min. In addition, with the exception of the experiments designed to characterize the purity of the beta+/betaC heterodimer discussed in the 'Results' section (Figure 3), SPR capture experiments utilized preparations of heterodimeric clamp that were supplemented with a 10-fold molar excess of the myc-tagged beta+ clamp, which is not recognized by the anti-Penta*His chip (Figure 2C). The presence of a molar excess of myc-tagged beta+ relative to the his6-tagged betaC (or beta+) clamp inhibited his6-tagged betaC (or beta+) homodimer formation, ensuring the purity of the heterodimer captured on the chip surface in SPR experiments. Figure 3.Interactions of the beta clamp with the delta subunit of DnaX. (A) Representative SPR results analyzing interaction of delta (4 muM) with various beta clamp proteins (approximately100 RU). Clamps examined include the his6-tagged beta+ homodimer (beta+), the his6-tagged betaC homodimer (betaC), the his6-tagged beta+/myc-tagged beta+ heterodimer (beta+/beta+) and the his6-tagged beta+/myc-tagged betaC heterodimer (beta+/betaC). Theoretical Rmax values for binding of a single delta subunit (delta1), or for two delta subunits (delta2) are indicated by dashed lines. (B) Titration of delta with his6-tagged beta+. Increasing concentrations of purified delta (0.1, 1, 10, 25, 50, 100, 250, 500, 750 and 1000 nM) were injected over approximately100 RU of his6-tagged beta+ captured on the anti-Penta*His chip surface. (C) Comparison of sensorgrams from low (25 nM) and high (1 microM) concentrations of delta injected over approximately100 RU of his6-tagged beta+ captured on the anti-Penta*His chip surface from panel (B) highlighting differences in association (ka) and dissociation rates (kd). The amplitudes (Rmax) of these SPR traces were normalized to each other to facilitate a direct comparison of the association and dissociation phases of the curves. (D) Titration of delta with beta+/betaC. Increasing concentrations of purified delta (10, 25, 50, 100 and 500 nM) were injected over approximately100 RU of beta+/betaC captured on the anti-Penta*His chip surface. (E) The double-injection experiment was performed as outlined in the cartoon (bottom). approximately100 RU of his6-tagged beta+ was captured on the anti-Penta*His chip surface (injection 1). Saturating levels (10 microM) of delta were injected (injection 2), forming the beta-delta2 complex (Rmax approaching 100 RU). Flow was switched to buffer, allowing for the more loosely bound delta (delta2) to dissociate, resulting in a beta-delta1 complex. Increasing concentrations of delta (0, 1, 10, 25, 50, 100, 250, 500, 750 and 1000 nM) were then injected over the beta-delta1 complex (injection 3) to determine the binding affinity for the second delta (delta2). (F) Summary table of kinetic constants for various clamp-delta interactions derived from SPR results summarized in (A-E). Results shown for beta+-delta were determined using the his6-tagged beta+ homodimer, and were indistinguishable from those observed with beta+/beta+. An interaction of betaC with delta was not detected (nd).
