The his<sub>6</sub>-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup>/myc-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup> form of &#946;<sup>+</sup> was used as the positive control (&#946;<sup>+</sup>/&#946;<sup>+</sup>). Results shown represent the average of three separate determinations. Error bars represent the standard deviation.</p></caption>
###xml 1354 1354 1297 1297 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xlink:href="gkp128f10"/>
###xml 961 1354 931 1297 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F10" position="float"><label>Figure 10.</label><caption><p textid="66">Ability of &#946;<sup>+</sup>, &#946;<sup>C</sup>, and &#946;<sup>+</sup>/&#946;<sup>C</sup> to stimulate Pol III* replication <italic>in vitro</italic>. Replication activity was measured as described in &#8216;Materials and Methods&#8217; section. The his<sub>6</sub>-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup>/myc-tagged &#946;<sup>+</sup> form of &#946;<sup>+</sup> was used as the positive control (&#946;<sup>+</sup>/&#946;<sup>+</sup>). Results shown represent the average of three separate determinations. Error bars represent the standard deviation.</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="gkp128f10"/></fig>
In light of our finding that the beta+/betaC heterodimer was proficient for interaction with DnaX and Pol IIIalpha, and was efficiently loaded onto DNA by DnaX, we asked whether it was capable of stimulating Pol III* replication in vitro using a primer extension assay. Replication activity in this assay is a reflection of both clamp loading and Pol IIIalpha-clamp interactions, which contribute to Pol III* processivity. As summarized in Figure 10, Pol III* was unable to synthesize significant levels of DNA in the absence of clamp. Likewise, betaC was unable to stimulate Pol III* replication (Figure 10), due to the fact that it was severely impaired for interactions with both the DnaX and Pol IIIalpha components of Pol III*. In contrast, addition of beta+ or beta+/betaC stimulated Pol III* replication to similar extents (Figure 10). These findings demonstrate that a single cleft in the clamp is sufficient to stimulate Pol III* replication in vitro. Figure 10.Ability of beta+, betaC, and beta+/betaC to stimulate Pol III* replication in vitro. Replication activity was measured as described in 'Materials and Methods' section. The his6-tagged beta+/myc-tagged beta+ form of beta+ was used as the positive control (beta+/beta+). Results shown represent the average of three separate determinations. Error bars represent the standard deviation.
###end p 65
###begin p 66
###xml 15 16 12 13 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>

###xml 33 34 24 25 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 39 40 27 28 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 75 83 63 71 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 175 176 163 164 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">6</sub>
###xml 188 189 173 174 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>

###begin title 67
DISCUSSION
###end title 67
###begin p 68
###xml 71 78 71 78 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 107 115 104 112 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F2">Figure 2</xref>
###xml 162 163 156 157 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 168 169 159 160 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 193 194 181 182 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 224 225 209 210 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 360 368 345 353 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 373 374 358 359 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F5">5</xref>
###xml 421 422 403 404 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 427 428 406 407 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 540 548 519 527 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F7">Figure 7</xref>
###xml 598 606 577 585 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 608 617 587 596 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F10">Figure 10</xref>
###xml 734 749 709 724 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F8 F9">Figures 8 and 9</xref>
###xml 773 774 745 746 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 779 780 748 749 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 818 819 784 785 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 841 849 807 815 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F7">Figure 7</xref>
###xml 1172 1173 1135 1136 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B9">9</xref>
###xml 1410 1411 1370 1371 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1416 1417 1373 1374 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 71 78 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
In this report, we describe a method for purification of heterodimeric E. coli beta siding clamp proteins (Figure 2). Using this method, we purified a clamp (beta+/betaC) consisting of the beta+ protomer in complex with betaC, which bears a mutant cleft that we demonstrated to be severely, if not completely impaired for interaction with the eubacterial CBM (Figure 3 and 5). Our biochemical characterization of the beta+/betaC heterodimer illustrated that a single cleft is sufficient for efficient loading of the clamp onto DNA by DnaX (Figure 7), as well as stimulation of Pol III* replication in vitro (Figure 10). In contrast, both clefts contributed to clamp unloading, regardless of whether it was catalyzed by DnaX or delta (Figures 8 and 9). Our finding that beta+/betaC was loaded more efficiently than beta+ (72% versus 46%; see Figure 7) may be the result of its reduced efficiency of unloading by DnaX. Regardless, these findings suggest that DnaX utilizes distinct mechanisms for loading and unloading clamps. Interactions of the clamp with the DNA template may underlie this difference. A crystal structure of the beta clamp on DNA was recently described (9). In the crystal, residues R24 and Q149, among others, interacted specifically with dsDNA. In addition, the cleft of the clamp interacted with ssDNA located downstream of the 3'-end of the primer. Thus, inefficient unloading of the beta+/betaC clamp catalyzed by DnaX may be due to competition between ssDNA and DnaX for the single cleft. Alternatively, interaction(s) of the clamp with DNA may contribute to unloading. Further work is required in order to test these hypotheses.
###end p 68
###begin p 69
###xml 137 144 130 137 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">monomer</sub>
###xml 200 202 186 188 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B39">39</xref>
###xml 428 429 396 397 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 434 435 399 400 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 493 501 454 462 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 639 641 596 598 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B18">18</xref>
###xml 642 644 599 601 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B32">32</xref>
###xml 668 676 625 633 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 1036 1044 981 989 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F9">Figure 9</xref>
In the crystal structure of residues 1-140 of delta in complex with a mutant form of the clamp that exists as a monomer in solution (betamonomer), residues L73-F74 of delta contacted the CBM of beta (39). Thus, the simplest interpretation of our results concerning interaction of delta with the beta clamp is that two delta subunits bind a single dimeric clamp, with each delta contacting a separate cleft. Our finding that beta+/betaC bound a single delta is consistent with this conclusion (Figure 3). It has been suggested that binding of delta to the clamp destabilizes the adjacent dimer interface, effecting clamp opening/unloading (18,32). Results discussed in Figure 3 demonstrate that binding of two delta subunits to a single clamp fails to dissociate the clamp into monomers. Thus, the ability of delta to open a dimer interface in the clamp appears to require more than it simply contacting the cleft region. This result, taken together with our finding that both clefts are required for delta to unload the clamp from DNA (Figure 9), suggests that two delta subunits may catalyze clamp unloading by simultaneously binding to both clefts and the DNA. In this way, delta could utilize energy from DNA binding to induce a conformational change in beta to open one, or possibly both dimer interfaces, effectively unloading the clamp from the DNA. Further work is required in order to test this model.
###end p 69
###begin p 70
###xml 227 234 212 219 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">monomer</sub>
###xml 326 328 307 309 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B39">39</xref>
Our observation that delta binds the clamp with negative cooperativity may be the result of steric clashing between the two delta subunits. Based on the crystal structure, it was argued that the positioning of delta on the betamonomer would preclude a second delta from binding the unoccupied cleft due to steric constraints (39). However, it should be stressed that the actual conformation of delta on a dimeric clamp, either in solution or encircling DNA is unknown. Alternatively, the observed negative cooperativity may result form conformational changes induced in the peptide backbone of the clamp upon binding of the first delta. These conformational changes may influence the structure of the second cleft, and/or other surfaces of the clamp involved in interactions with one or more specific partner proteins. An interesting aspect of this model is that partners could impose a plastic hierarchy in clamp-partner interactions. By inducing conformational changes in beta, binding of one partner to the clamp could dictate both the identity(ies) and the order in which additional partners are recruited. Clamp-DNA and partner-DNA interactions likely influence these interactions, as well, and thus contribute to the switching mechanism(s).
###end p 70
###begin p 71
Our finding that delta requires both clefts in order to unload clamp from DNA suggests that that a clamp in complex with a partner that is doing work on DNA is protected from unloading, while a clamp that is not in complex with a partner is efficiently unloaded from DNA by delta. Alternatively, since delta binds the clamp with negative cooperativity, it could effect displacement of a resident partner, provided it had access to one cleft, and was able to influence affinity of the partner bound to the other cleft. Although it would be counter productive to unload a clamp that was in complex with a partner that was doing work on DNA, it would be advantageous to unload a clamp that was in complex with a partner that was for some reason precluded from functioning properly, such as Pol III* stalled at a lesion. Conformational differences between a moving Pol III* and a stalled Pol III* may influence access of delta to the cleft of a beta clamp that is not in complex with Pol IIIalpha. Upon binding, delta may induce a conformational change in the polypeptide backbone of the beta clamp, which, in turn, may destabilize interaction of the clamp with Pol IIIalpha, leading to dissociation of Pol III*. This would allow for recruitment of either another Pol capable of catalyzing TLS through its association with clamp persisting on the DNA, or a second delta to effect unloading of the clamp. In the latter case, DnaX could then load a new clamp at the primer/template junction abutting the lesion for subsequent recruitment of a TLS Pol. Thus, delta and/or DnaX may influence Pol switching either directly, by disassembling clamp-Pol complexes stalled at lesions, or indirectly, by loading new clamps at primer/template junctions abutting lesions.
###end p 71
###begin p 72
###xml 25 26 22 23 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 31 32 25 26 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 76 77 67 68 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 141 150 132 141 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F10">Figure 10</xref>
###xml 595 597 579 581 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B44">44</xref>
###xml 767 768 744 745 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</sup>
###xml 800 808 777 785 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F6">Figure 6</xref>
###xml 1047 1055 1024 1032 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
Our finding that the beta+/betaC heterodimer was indistinguishable from beta+ with respect to its ability to stimulate Pol III* replication (Figure 10) indicates that processive replication by Pol III* requires that Pol IIIalpha interact with only a single cleft in the clamp. As such, these findings provide support for the toolbelt model, in which Pol III* is bound to one cleft, and a TLS Pol is bound to the second cleft. Alternatively, the structure of the Pol IV little finger domain in complex with the beta clamp, indicating that Pol IV contacts both the cleft and the rim of the clamp (44), suggests that surfaces of the clamp in addition to the cleft play roles in Pol function and/or switching. Our finding that Pol IIIalpha retained weak affinity for betaC is consistent with this model (Figure 6). Thus, Pol switching on the clamp may involve surfaces in addition to the cleft. Our method to purify heterodimeric clamp proteins will enable a detailed biochemical dissection of the mechanisms by which the clamp mediates Pol switching in vitro.
###end p 72
###begin p 73
###xml 76 78 64 66 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B45">45</xref>
###xml 116 117 92 93 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">7</sup>
###xml 264 265 228 229 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">5</sup>
###xml 294 296 258 260 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B46">46</xref>
###xml 554 556 506 508 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B47">47</xref>
###xml 0 5 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">Human</span>
###xml 432 437 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
Human PCNA remains on circular DNA with a half-life of approximately24 min (45). Since there are approximately2 x 107 Okazaki fragments synthesized on lagging strand during each replication cycle, assuming an average length of 200 bp, and there are approximately105 PCNA trimers in HeLa cells (46), it seems likely that eukaryotes recycle their clamps on average approximately100 times each during replication/repair synthesis. The human clamp loader complex, RFC, as well as the 40-kDa subunit of RFC, RFC2, are each capable of unloading PCNA from DNA (47). It will be interesting to determine whether interactions of RFC2 with PCNA involve negative cooperativity, as well as whether three RFC2 subunits are capable of binding a single PCNA clamp. Likewise, purification of differentially tagged heterotrimeric forms of PCNA would enable the determination of the number of clefts required for loading, processive replication by Pol delta and Pol epsilon, and unloading, as well as DNA repair and damage tolerance functions that are managed by the clamp.
###end p 73
###begin p 74
###xml 509 517 495 503 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in trans</italic>
In summary, findings discussed in this report indicate that interaction of the beta clamp with delta, as well as possibly other partner proteins, is far more complex than previously appreciated. Further work is required in order to understand the mechanistic basis for the observed negative cooperativity between beta and delta. Likewise, it will be interesting to determine whether additional partners interact with the clamp through positive or negative cooperativity, and whether cooperativity is observed in trans between different partners. Although the goal of the work discussed in this report was focused largely on determining the roles of the clefts in clamp loading, Pol III* replication and clamp unloading/recycling, our method for purification of heterodimeric clamp proteins will facilitate detailed biochemical dissection of the mechanistic roles of the clamp in DNA replication and repair, as well as in coordinating the actions of its multiple partners on the DNA during DNA replication, repair and TLS.
###end p 74
